    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

<span id="freeTextContainer4036463778030534381">Hubert Selby is probably one of the six best novelists writing in the English language.?<i>Financial Times</i><br/><br/>Author of controversial cult classic, <i>Last Exit to Brooklyn</i>, Hubert Selby began as a writer of short fiction. He excels in this form, plunging the reader head-first into the densely realized worlds of his protagonists, in which the details of daily life rub shoulders with</span>
  <span id="freeText4036463778030534381" style="display:none">Hubert Selby is probably one of the six best novelists writing in the English language.?<i>Financial Times</i><br /><br />Author of controversial cult classic, <i>Last Exit to Brooklyn</i>, Hubert Selby began as a writer of short fiction. He excels in this form, plunging the reader head-first into the densely realized worlds of his protagonists, in which the details of daily life rub shoulders with obsession and madness.<br /><br />Although fundamentally concerned with morality, Selby's own sense of humility prevents him from preaching. He offers instead a passionate empathy with the ordinary dreams and aspirations of his characters, a brilliant ear for the urban vernacular and for the voices of conscience and self-deceit that torment his characters.<br /><br />"A major American author of a stature with William Burroughs and Joseph Heller."?<i>Los Angeles Times</i><br /><br />"Selby's place is in the front rank of American novelists ... to understand his work is to understand the anguish of America."?<i>The New York Times Book Review</i><br /><br />Novels by <b>Hubert Selby Jr</b> available from Marion Boyars: <i>Last Exit to Brooklyn, The Room, The Demon, Requiem for a Dream</i> and <i>The Willow Tree</i>..</span>
  <a data-text-id="4036463778030534381" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_692911088" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/692911088" />
    <a title="Ryland Dinneen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6616133-ryland-dinneen"><img alt="Ryland Dinneen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1591407680p2/6616133.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/692911088?book_show_action=true">Aug 12, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ryland Dinneen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ryland Dinneen" href="/user/show/6616133-ryland-dinneen">Ryland Dinneen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6616133-ryland-dinneen?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6616133-ryland-dinneen?shelf=contemporary">contemporary</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer692911088" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13877966907532056909">I'm very surprised that SOTSN doesn't seem to be a popular one for Selby. I personally loved every page of it, and some of the stories really left me feeling raw. Hubert's writing isn't the same as always - he still goes for the brutally realistic and the disturbing, but here he has mixed it up with stories that are far more tender to the touch. His writing is always full of love, no matter how hellish the situations can be, but the love shines through the pages here like it hasn't in any of his</span>
  <span id="freeText13877966907532056909" style="display:none">I'm very surprised that SOTSN doesn't seem to be a popular one for Selby. I personally loved every page of it, and some of the stories really left me feeling raw. Hubert's writing isn't the same as always - he still goes for the brutally realistic and the disturbing, but here he has mixed it up with stories that are far more tender to the touch. His writing is always full of love, no matter how hellish the situations can be, but the love shines through the pages here like it hasn't in any of his books before. He writes beautifully, delicately, dealing with the downtrodden and the misunderstood with tenderness and caring, no matter what happens to them. As opposed to the standard Selby fare (disturbing, violent, grisly), there are a few stories that really shine with sentimentality that never gets so overdone as to become cloying. Notable stories would include Of Whales and Dreams, and Song of the Silent Snow, which I think was my personal favourite. The imagery is vivid and fantastic, but the character is filled with emotional trauma just begging to be tended.<br /><br />This is probably my shittiest book review ever, but I really loved this book. It's just beautiful, I have no other words. Read it whether you like Selby or not.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13877966907532056909" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3375560853" />
    <a title="Steven Godin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/26706841-steven-godin"><img alt="Steven Godin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605181037p2/26706841.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3375560853?book_show_action=true">Jun 16, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Steven Godin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Steven Godin" href="/user/show/26706841-steven-godin">Steven Godin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer5267535152553094543">I thought this was a novel when it is in fact a collection of short stories. Anyone familiar with Selby Jr. novels won't be surprised to hear that he covers the same sort of ground here; with some hard-hitting, angst, and despairing writing that digs deep into the psyche of its characters, who are mostly the kind of folk that society would tend to ignore. Not as powerful or gut-wrenching as Requiem for a Dream or Last exit to Brooklyn, but Selby Jr. rarely gives the reader easy ride here. His po</span>
  <span id="freeText5267535152553094543" style="display:none">I thought this was a novel when it is in fact a collection of short stories. Anyone familiar with Selby Jr. novels won't be surprised to hear that he covers the same sort of ground here; with some hard-hitting, angst, and despairing writing that digs deep into the psyche of its characters, who are mostly the kind of folk that society would tend to ignore. Not as powerful or gut-wrenching as Requiem for a Dream or Last exit to Brooklyn, but Selby Jr. rarely gives the reader easy ride here. His portrait of gritty realism is at times second to none. Favourite stories were - Hi Champ, Fortune Cookie, A Penny For Your Thoughts, The Sound, Song Of The Silent Snow.</span>

<div id="review_591337024" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/591337024" />
    <a title="MJ Nicholls" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2386804-mj-nicholls"><img alt="MJ Nicholls" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1374341850p2/2386804.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/591337024?book_show_action=true">Apr 15, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="MJ Nicholls" class="user" itemprop="url" name="MJ Nicholls" href="/user/show/2386804-mj-nicholls">MJ Nicholls</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780241951248" href="/book/show/14848334-song-of-the-silent-snow">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2386804-mj-nicholls?shelf=merkins">merkins</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2386804-mj-nicholls?shelf=penguin-classics">penguin-classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2386804-mj-nicholls?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer591337024" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7741615213215429623">HSJ’s only story collection, released in 1986, lacks the power of his novel work—those books thrive on slow-building doom and the repetitive grind of addiction and madness, whereas these vignettes can’t attribute their weaknesses to style. Selby’s affinity and loyalty to the down-and-outs of New York never relented, unlike Lou Reed, who switched from bourgeois reformist to street-smart wiseguy in the space of two albums (listen to <i>New York</i> then play the laughable <i>The Blue Mask</i> to see my point). </span>
  <span id="freeText7741615213215429623" style="display:none">HSJ’s only story collection, released in 1986, lacks the power of his novel work—those books thrive on slow-building doom and the repetitive grind of addiction and madness, whereas these vignettes can’t attribute their weaknesses to style. Selby’s affinity and loyalty to the down-and-outs of New York never relented, unlike Lou Reed, who switched from bourgeois reformist to street-smart wiseguy in the space of two albums (listen to <i>New York</i> then play the laughable <i>The Blue Mask</i> to see my point). Like Reed’s 80s lyrics, some of the material here is nigh-unreadable: Selby makes Writing-101 mistakes in stories like horror schlock ‘The Sound’ or the diabolical mess ‘Liebesnacht.’ A more direct approach suits the form, with the epistolary ‘Im Being Good’ and ‘Indian Summer’ among the stronger hits—surprising shocks of reality from lives barely held together with lint-fluffed sticky tape. But Selby’s style (and inability to advance beyond that style) simply doesn’t satisfy in the same way as those masterpieces, and Selby’s standards are lightyears beyond the writing in evidence. </span>
  <a data-text-id="7741615213215429623" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Julian Meynell" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Julian Meynell" href="/user/show/12278245-julian-meynell">Julian Meynell</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12278245-julian-meynell?shelf=american">american</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12278245-julian-meynell?shelf=20th-century">20th-century</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12278245-julian-meynell?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer450019281" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3158545355917447470">This is a collection of short stories. As with all collections of short stories it varies in quality. It does not have the same intensity as the very best of Selby's writings, but some of it is really very good. He is at his best when he is exploring the intimate horrors of the lowest dregs of society in the tales. I find Selby to be always right on the money when he does this. He is the most accurate person on the life in the gutter of America. His writing here has its brilliance and subtlety b</span>
  <span id="freeText3158545355917447470" style="display:none">This is a collection of short stories. As with all collections of short stories it varies in quality. It does not have the same intensity as the very best of Selby's writings, but some of it is really very good. He is at his best when he is exploring the intimate horrors of the lowest dregs of society in the tales. I find Selby to be always right on the money when he does this. He is the most accurate person on the life in the gutter of America. His writing here has its brilliance and subtlety but it is not as full of rage as his best work. My book's introduction compared him to Dante which is a very intelligent comparison.<br /><br />As always, he is a deeply Christian and ethical writer. He has a remarkable compassion and empathy which I think goes far beyond almost any writer and it is the most striking thing about his work. A great but uneven collection, which confirms my opinion that Selby is one of the most unjustly neglected writers in history.<br /><br />Beautiful, horrific and true.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3158545355917447470" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/332561-louis-zieja?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer5463139" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12066355053497763738">At times crude, at times poetic, often trancendent. My favorite kind of short story collection: one that reads better as a complete work, a novel of unconnected characters.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer29238231" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9468875974131659165">This collection of stories is excellent, notably "The Coat". The protagonists are all named Harry or Harold, with only one exception. At first I thought they all referred to the same person, but one or two of the stories make that seem unlikely. Reading about it on damazon, I came across an angry exchange between two reviewers: one complained on the Harry thing and the other said the complainer had "missed the point," implying some arcane significance to them all being Harry that was obvious to </span>
  <span id="freeText9468875974131659165" style="display:none">This collection of stories is excellent, notably "The Coat". The protagonists are all named Harry or Harold, with only one exception. At first I thought they all referred to the same person, but one or two of the stories make that seem unlikely. Reading about it on damazon, I came across an angry exchange between two reviewers: one complained on the Harry thing and the other said the complainer had "missed the point," implying some arcane significance to them all being Harry that was obvious to the ins. Hmmm, the protag from Requiem For A Dream is named Harry--I think one of the main guys in Last Exit to Brooklyn is, too. So maybe there is some weird point! Anyway, it's a nice touch, whatever it stands for. </span>
  <a data-text-id="9468875974131659165" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_771464340" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="Allan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/25891345-allan"><img alt="Allan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1522446840p2/25891345.jpg" /></a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer771464340" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17120904931968108205">I've read all of Selby Jr's novels, and have to say that I enjoyed them more than I did this collection. Selby's work can be hard hitting thematically and challenging to read stylistically, and while this approach was effective at times in this collection, at other times it seemed a little forced. Not a book that I'd consider revisiting and I would recommend investing time in his novels rather than this collection should anyone wish to read his work. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2648779606" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2648779606" />
    <a title="Zac Johnson" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/431978-zac-johnson"><img alt="Zac Johnson" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1468886470p2/431978.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2648779606?book_show_action=true">Dec 31, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Zac Johnson" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Zac Johnson" href="/user/show/431978-zac-johnson">Zac Johnson</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2648779606" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11232819372000548094">Hubert Selby Jr. is responsible for one of my favorite novels, the soul-shaking Requiem for a Dream. There are some glimmers of that brilliance contained in this short story collection, such as the titular tale, but there are far more misfires that only half work than there are masterpieces. Every character, for instance, is named Harry. Is this the same Harry in each story? Is it the Harry from his novels? Is Selby just really lazy when it comes to naming things? Who knows. <br/><br/>What I do know is th</span>
  <span id="freeText11232819372000548094" style="display:none">Hubert Selby Jr. is responsible for one of my favorite novels, the soul-shaking Requiem for a Dream. There are some glimmers of that brilliance contained in this short story collection, such as the titular tale, but there are far more misfires that only half work than there are masterpieces. Every character, for instance, is named Harry. Is this the same Harry in each story? Is it the Harry from his novels? Is Selby just really lazy when it comes to naming things? Who knows. <br /><br />What I do know is the collection is still worthwhile for:<br />Song of the Silent Snow: an absolute masterpiece that deserves to be regarded among the best short stories of all time<br />Fortune Cookie: Also turned into a movie by the same director, Darren Aronofsky, as Requiem for a Dream.<br />And, showing promise even if they don't have the same force we expect from the author:<br />Liebesnacht and Double Feature.<br /><br />The rest, at the very least, didn't move me. But Selby set a high bar for himself, and not everything a genius writes is going to be genius itself.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11232819372000548094" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer2493787363799564040">Selby, chronicler of the downtrodden, often depressed/unhinged/pathetic underbelly of society, returns in this collection of stories to portray the dashed dreams and struggles of men and women in NYC in the 70s. <br/><br/><br/><br/>There are some interesting and sad stories here, particularly “the Coat,” and Selby capably renders a sense of atmosphere and tragedy, while still adding a touch of dark humor. <br/><br/><br/><br/>Nevertheless, these stories lack the visceral punch and insanity of some of his other works. Selby does not see</span>
  <span id="freeText2493787363799564040" style="display:none">Selby, chronicler of the downtrodden, often depressed/unhinged/pathetic underbelly of society, returns in this collection of stories to portray the dashed dreams and struggles of men and women in NYC in the 70s. <br /><br /><br /><br />There are some interesting and sad stories here, particularly “the Coat,” and Selby capably renders a sense of atmosphere and tragedy, while still adding a touch of dark humor. <br /><br /><br /><br />Nevertheless, these stories lack the visceral punch and insanity of some of his other works. Selby does not seem to be reaching into the dark soul of humanity so much as wandering around its edges. <br /><br /><br /><br />Still, it is a decent collection and a quick read.<br /><br /></span>

<div id="review_3544663001" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3544663001" />
    <a title="Jackson" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/145196-jackson"><img alt="Jackson" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1227825849p2/145196.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3544663001?book_show_action=true">Sep 12, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jackson" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jackson" href="/user/show/145196-jackson">Jackson</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3544663001" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6771753386411503506">I began reading this book in July 2008. I got approximately a third of the way through it, maybe a bit more. The thing I remember most about it was taking it in my backpack for an overnight trip in Glacier National Park to Lincoln Lake, a somewhat obscure destination, at least by GNP standards. It was about seven or eight miles through thick forest to the campsite from the pull-off on the Going-to-the-Sun Road on the west side of the park. The hike took a long time, the day was foggy, the trail </span>
  <span id="freeText6771753386411503506" style="display:none">I began reading this book in July 2008. I got approximately a third of the way through it, maybe a bit more. The thing I remember most about it was taking it in my backpack for an overnight trip in Glacier National Park to Lincoln Lake, a somewhat obscure destination, at least by GNP standards. It was about seven or eight miles through thick forest to the campsite from the pull-off on the Going-to-the-Sun Road on the west side of the park. The hike took a long time, the day was foggy, the trail was wet, and visibility was bad due to tall underbrush that grew up to my face. The whole time I was very nervous about encountering a bear.<br /><br />The campground is beautiful, and I was the only one there. But the tent area sat tucked into tall underbrush and weeds, eliminating the view a bear would have of me (or I would have of it) until it was right upon me. And a tall waterfall cascaded into the lake (dropping down from Avalanche Lake), and though it was across the lake from me, the sound was loud and incessant. I couldn't see far, and I couldn't hear anything, and I was alone in bear country. Not something I was unaccustomed to, or generally fearful of, but being alone and stationary for about 18 hours in this place was a little unsettling. (And the next morning, after a drizzling overnight rain, the mud around the lake and on the trail was riddled with fresh bear pawprints.)<br /><br />After dinner, I set up my tent, lit a candle, and read a chunk of this book. Selby is probably not the best author to read while you are feeling unsettled, but for Selby, this book is mild. In fact, compared to masterpieces like <em>Requiem For a Dream</em> and <em>Last Exit to Brooklyn</em>, it is downright tame. I put it down when I returned to my apartment in Columbia Falls and never picked it up again.<br /><br />That is, until recently. Twelve-plus years later I have a daughter and am far away from Montana. I was picking up around her dollhouse, which sits in front of the bottom shelf of one of my bookcases, and found this poking out, covered in dust, with the bookmark still stuck where I left off in the woods a long time ago. So I thought, <em>What the hell</em>, and determined to finish it.<br /><br />It was a slog. A number of pieces here are meditations on the dreary, soul-draining nature of working nine-to-five and the numbing routine that comes along with it. But, for the most part, it fails to hit hard. My favorite pieces are the very short essay "Of Whales and Dreams" and the story "The Coat," about a homeless man and his coat, the only item on earth, animate or inanimate, that brings him any security or comfort. <br /><br />But that's about it. Otherwise, this is a book that misses its mark. If Selby hadn't already published masterpieces prior to this book, I doubt it would have seen the light of day. Without his name attached to it, it fails to stand on its own, and is only recommended for those who wish to read every word he ever wrote.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6771753386411503506" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Kurishin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17355202-kurishin"><img alt="Kurishin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1588663085p2/17355202.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

              <span id="reviewTextContainer569133792" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2098553987411260454">There are some brilliant short stories in this collection but the overall is let down by some duds (in an attempt to publish 15 stories, I presume). Selby gets at places Cheever can't or won't in suburbia and the NYC pieces are generally excellent. The pieces that lack tension and movement unsurprisingly leave one wishing they were excluded. I would rather have this variance than other, more steady collections at a flatline three stars.</span>

          </span>

<div id="review_50859864" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/50859864" />
    <a title="Evan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1248986-evan"><img alt="Evan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1488574589p2/1248986.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/50859864?book_show_action=true">Mar 29, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Evan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Evan" href="/user/show/1248986-evan">Evan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1248986-evan?shelf=currently-reading-slowly">currently-reading-slowly</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer50859864&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer50859864" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12186936203917952466">Short vignettes about life in the city... I thought this might be a nice volume to take up to Subway so that I could polish off a few short items without becoming committed to a long novel during lunch. <br/><br/>These stories of ordinary people doing ordinary things have a lot more heart (so far) than Selby's unremittingly grim "Last Exit to Brooklyn." <br/><br/>The second story, "Hi Champ," has some Runyonesque charm but veers into some uncomfortable insights.<br/>The story's protagonist, Harry, is having a first date</span>
  <span id="freeText12186936203917952466" style="display:none">Short vignettes about life in the city... I thought this might be a nice volume to take up to Subway so that I could polish off a few short items without becoming committed to a long novel during lunch. <br /><br />These stories of ordinary people doing ordinary things have a lot more heart (so far) than Selby's unremittingly grim "Last Exit to Brooklyn." <br /><br />The second story, "Hi Champ," has some Runyonesque charm but veers into some uncomfortable insights.<br />The story's protagonist, Harry, is having a first date with Rita and wants to find a way to impress her. At Jack Dempsey's restaurant he summons the courage to ask the owner, the champ himself, for a favor, and instantly the gregarious pugilist knows right off what Harry wants (he's been this road before..) Sure, I could pretend to know you and call you by your name during your date tomorrow to impress your girl.<br />When Dempsey greets Harry the next night by name, the intended effect occurs. Rita is impressed.<br />After an incredible, perfect date, Harry begins to wrestle with issues perhaps best left alone, he wonders if the seemingly harmless lie he fabricates to impress his date is simply the first of many, and whether, thus, the whole relationship is built on a lie from the outset. They get along famously, and maybe they would have done so without the lie, but maybe they wouldn't. Was it the lie that she dug, or him? Without having alternate lives to compare the outcomes, how can one know?<br /><br />reading on...<br /><br />"A Penny for Your Thoughts." A married man harbors fantasies about a young girl he sees every day at the train station, but knows nothing will come of it. He loves his wife. Realizes that he knows the color of the girl's eyes but can't remember the color of his wife's...<br /><br />"Fortune Cookie." A salesman on a streak of bad luck and sensing his imminent dismissal gets on a winning path with a series of positive fortune cookies. But fear of breaking the cycle forces him to eat Chinese every day and he starts getting sick. He tries to find a way to get fortune cookies without eating Chinese all the time. But then the fortunes start being ambiguous or negative... The ritual becomes a kind of self-fulfilling prophecy...<br /><br />reading on...<br /> <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="12186936203917952466" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1563155813" />

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1563155813?book_show_action=true">Feb 28, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sarah Tipper" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sarah Tipper" href="/user/show/17905900-sarah-tipper">Sarah Tipper</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1563155813" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6848879881955184150">For the first couple of stories I found the punctuation distracting but then I stopped noticing. The depth of Selby’s characters is wonderful, you feel you’re right behind their eyes, on the inside looking out. Some of his characters are the sort of people who don’t usually get a voice. Selby covers depression, homelessness, frustrated fathers and many more characters in the fifteen short stories in this book. Sometimes you’re surprised at the places people find joy. It reminded me a little of A</span>
  <span id="freeText6848879881955184150" style="display:none">For the first couple of stories I found the punctuation distracting but then I stopped noticing. The depth of Selby’s characters is wonderful, you feel you’re right behind their eyes, on the inside looking out. Some of his characters are the sort of people who don’t usually get a voice. Selby covers depression, homelessness, frustrated fathers and many more characters in the fifteen short stories in this book. Sometimes you’re surprised at the places people find joy. It reminded me a little of A Streetcar Named Desire, Selby’s men leap from the page full of energy (which is sometimes destructive energy) like Stanley Kowalski does. I love the positive ending of the story Song Of The Silent Snow. </span>
  <a data-text-id="6848879881955184150" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('S6eLEWc9rANRlRUhqskK4c70kvMEM2OQ1h7qBVB4yeDt+BcyyXwXWJ7Bh26AIn/0kjuL6STvPKwfcxXKyismXw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('oZWRMdYIuJerBO4S6XNVjZ/U9MpchEz/tlyP95dhZc0Hyg0SeEkDzGRQfF3DmCCYwxvt0HxYE8N/MXA4DTKKcg==')})
  }

Four boys becoming men: Juice Terry, the work-shy fanny-merchant, with corks<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText17082840502992502201\" style=\"display:none\">Glue is the story of four boys growing up in the Edinburgh schemes, and about the loyalties, the experiences - and the secrets - that hold them together into their thirties. Four boys becoming men: Juice Terry, the work-shy fanny-merchant, with corkscrew curls and sticky fingers; Billy the boxer: driven, controlled, playing to his strengths; Carl, the Milky Bar Kid, drifting along to his own soundtrack; and the doomed Gally - who has one less skin than everyone else and seems to find catastrophe at every corner. As we follow their lives from the seventies into the new century - from punk to techno, from speed to Es - we can see each of them trying to struggle out from under the weight of the conditioning of class and culture, peer pressure and their parents\' hopes that maybe their sons will do better than they did. What binds the four of them is the friendship formed by the scheme, their school, and their ambition to escape from both; their loyalty fused in street morality: back up your mates, don\'t hit women and, most importantly, never grass - on anyone. Despite its scale and ambition, Glue has all Irvine Welsh\'s usual pace and vigour, crackling dialogue, scabrous set-pieces and black, black humour, but it is also a grown-up book about growing up - about the way we live our lives, and what happens to us when things become unstuck.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"17082840502992502201\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return Burroughs, es al mismo tiempo un descarnado autorretrato narrativo, una historia de amor brutalmente realista, una g<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText291960540926141653\" style=\"display:none\">Escrita en 1952 pero no publicada hasta 1985 debido a su franca plasmación del deseo homosexual, &quot;Queer&quot;, obra temprana de William S. Burroughs, es al mismo tiempo un descarnado autorretrato narrativo, una historia de amor brutalmente realista, una grotesca fantasía tragicómica y una ingeniosa novela política. Un libro que proporciona muchas claves fundamentales para adentrarse en el arrollador universo literario del autor. Esta edición definitiva, editada con motivo del 25 aniversario de su primera publicación, incorpora una extensa y documentada introducción de Oliver Harris en la que se repasan las complejas circunstancias personales en las que la obra fue escrita y que marcarían la vida y la posterior trayectoria literaria de Burroughs. Y se incluye también, a modo de epílogo, el texto que el propio autor escribió como prólogo para la edición de 1985.\n\n&quot;Queer&quot; está ambientada en un inmenso suburbio, que Burroughs definiría más tarde como la «Interzona», y que abarca desde la Ciudad de México, capital mundial del delito, hasta Panamá. Un álter ego del escritor, Lee, teje su tela amorosa en torno a Allerton, un joven ambiguo, indiferente como un animal. Deambula por locales cada vez más sórdidos, en los que pulula una fauna en estado de descomposición, y en esas excursiones, como un pícaro alienado, nos regala astillas radiactivas de su negrísimo humor. Para resolver sus obsesiones mortíferas y sexuales, Lee parte con su amigo a la búsqueda de la ayahuasca, droga absoluta capaz de otorgar el control total sobre los cerebros, y por eso mismo codiciada por Rusia y Estados Unidos... y por todo adicto. Dispuesto a abismarse en todos los peligros, como un santo o un criminal con orden de búsqueda y captura, Lee no tiene nada que perder. En esta novela aflora por primera vez ese paisaje alucinado que hoy todo lector reconoce como el mundo particular de William S.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/26335.Hubert_Selby_Jr_">About Hubert Selby Jr.</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/26335.Hubert_Selby_Jr_"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1397502493p3/26335.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/26335.Hubert_Selby_Jr_">Hubert Selby Jr.
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
1,964
followers
</div>

</div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer17177578908611763363">Hubert Selby, Jr. was born in Brooklyn and went to sea as a merchant marine while still in his teens. Laid low by lung disease, he was, after a decade of hospitalizations, written off as a goner and sent home to die. Deciding instead to live, but having no way to make a living, he came to a realization that would change the course of literature: "I knew the alphabet. Maybe I could be a writer." Dr</span>
  <span id="freeText17177578908611763363" style="display:none">Hubert Selby, Jr. was born in Brooklyn and went to sea as a merchant marine while still in his teens. Laid low by lung disease, he was, after a decade of hospitalizations, written off as a goner and sent home to die. Deciding instead to live, but having no way to make a living, he came to a realization that would change the course of literature: "I knew the alphabet. Maybe I could be a writer." Drawing from the soul of his Brooklyn neighborhood, he began writing something called "The Queen Is Dead," which evolved, after six years, into his first novel, <i>Last Exit to Brooklyn</i> (1964), a book that Allen Ginsberg predicted would "explode like a rusty hellish bombshell over America and still be eagerly read in a hundred years."<br /><br />Selby's second novel, <i>The Room</i> (1971), considered by some to be his masterpiece, received, as Selby said, "the greatest reviews I've ever read in my life," then rapidly vanished leaving barely a trace of its existence. Over the years, however, especially in Europe, <i>The Room</i> has come to be recognized as what Selby himself perceives it to be: the most disturbing book ever written, a book that he himself was unable to read again for twenty years after writing it.<br /><br />"A man obsessed / is a man possessed / by a demon." Thus the defining epigraph of <i>The Demon</i> (1976), a novel that, like <i>The Room</i>, has been better understood and more widely embraced abroad than at home.<br /><br />If <i>The Room</i> is Selby's own favorite among his books, <i>Requiem for a Dream</i> (1978) contains his favorite opening line: "Harry locked his mother in the closet." It is perhaps the truest and most horrific tale of heroin addiction ever written. <br /><br /><i>Song of the Silent Snow</i> (1986) brought together fifteen stories whose writing spanned more than twenty years.<br /><br />Selby continued to write short fiction, screenplays and teleplays at his apartment in West Hollywood. His work appeared in many journals, including <i>Yugen, Black Mountain Review, Evergreen Review, Provincetown Review, Kulchur, New Directions Annual, Swank</i> and <i>Open City</i>. For the last 20 years of his life, Selby taught creative writing as an adjunct professor in the Master of Professional Writing program at the University of Southern California. Selby often wryly noted that The New York Times would not review his books when they were published, but he predicted that they'd print his obituary.<br /><br />The movie <i>Last Exit to Brooklyn,</i> Directed by Uli Edel, was made in 1989 and his 1978 novel <i>Requiem for a Dream</i> was made into a film that was released in 2000. Selby himself had a small role as a prison guard. <br /><br />In the 1980s, Selby made the acquaintance of rock singer Henry Rollins, who had long admired Selby's works and publicly championed them. Rollins not only helped broaden Selby's readership, but also arranged recording sessions and reading tours for Selby. Rollins issued original recordings through his own 2.13.61 publications, and distributed Selby's other works.<br /><br />During the last years of his life, Selby suffered from depression and fits of rage, but was always a caring father and grandfather. The last month of his life Selby spent in and out of the hospital. He died in Highland Park, Los Angeles, California of chronic obstructive pulmonary lung disease. Selby was survived by his wife of 35 years, Suzanne; four children and 11 grandchildren.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17177578908611763363" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/49119-song-of-the-silent-snow">Quotes from Song of the Silen...</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;He looked down at the street, and the unbroken whiteness, and watched his foot touch the snow and listened to the slight crunching sound as he stepped forward. He looked back at his footprints. They were fascinating. He had been the only one to walk along this street today. There wasn’t even the mark of a dog or squirrel, or the scratch of a bird. He continued through the soft, silent snow, a feeling of peace starting to flow through him, helping make his step lighter and easier.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/8157184-he-looked-down-at-the-street-and-the-unbroken-whiteness">18 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;He looked at the houses he had been passing these weeks and though he had never studied them carefully they had become familiar through the process of seeing them so often, and he was now impressed with the change in their appearance as he looked at them through the gray of the air and whiteness of the snow, each house, shrub, tree, bush and mailbox trimmed with snow and blending into the air as if they were just a picture projected upon the still, pearly grayness, just an impression created by the silent snow, a picture on the edge and verge of disappearing and leaving only the air and snow through which he now lightly walked.<br />It did not seem possible, but the air was even softer and quieter. He continued walking alongside his prints feeling he could walk forever, that as long as the silent snow continued falling he could continue walking, and as he did he would leave behind all worries and cares, all horrors of the past and future. There would be nothing to bother him or torture his mind and fill his body with tremors of fear, the dark night of the soul over. There would only be himself and the soft, silent snow; and each flake, in its own life, its own separate and distinct entity, would bring with it its own joy, and he would easily partake of that joy as he continued walking, the gentle, silent snow falling ever so quietly, ever so joyously ... yes, and ever so love-ing-ly ... loveing-ly....&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/8157190-he-looked-at-the-houses-he-had-been-passing-these">18 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
        <a class="actionLink" style="float: right" href="/work/quotes/49119-song-of-the-silent-snow">More quotes&hellip;</a>
